<p>Common ravens have a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Bird_vocalization" title="Bird vocalization">vocalizations</a> which are of interest to <a href="/wiki/Ornithologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ornithologist">ornithologists</a>. Gwinner carried out important studies in the early 1960s, recording and photographing his findings in great detail.<sup id="cite_ref-Gwinner64_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gwinner64-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Fifteen to 30 categories of vocalization have been recorded for this species, most of which are used for social interaction. Calls recorded include alarm calls, chase calls, and flight calls. The species has a distinctive, deep, resonant <i>prruk-prruk-prruk</i> call, which to experienced listeners is unlike that of any other corvid. Its very wide and complex vocabulary includes a high, knocking <i>toc-toc-toc</i>, a dry, grating <i>kraa</i>, a low guttural rattle and some calls of an almost musical nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Gwinner64_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gwinner64-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like other corvids, the common raven can mimic sounds from their environment, including human speech. Non-vocal sounds produced by the common raven include wing whistles and bill snapping. Clapping or clicking has been observed more often in females than in males. If a member of a pair is lost, its mate reproduces the calls of its lost partner to encourage its return.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg/220px-Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg/330px-Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg/440px-Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_corax_jouveniles.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two juveniles in Iceland</div></div></div>
<p>The common raven can thrive in varied climates; indeed this species has the largest range of any member of the genus,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helm_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helm-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and one of the largest of any passerine.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> They range throughout the <a href="/wiki/Holarctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Holarctic">Holarctic</a> from Arctic and <a href="/wiki/Temperate" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate">temperate</a> habitats in North America and Eurasia to the deserts of North Africa, and to islands in the Pacific Ocean. In the British Isles, they are more common in Scotland, Wales, northern England and the west of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-observerbook_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-observerbook-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, they have been recorded at altitudes up to 5,000&#160;m (16,400&#160;ft), and as high as 6,350&#160;m (20,600&#160;ft) on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Helm_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helm-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The population sometimes known as the Punjab raven—described as <i>Corvus corax laurencei</i> (also spelt <i>lawrencii</i> or <i>laurencii</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Allan_Octavian_Hume" title="Allan Octavian Hume">Allan Octavian Hume</a> but more often considered synonymous with <i>subcorax</i><sup id="cite_ref-pcr_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcr-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>—is restricted to the <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> district of Pakistan and adjoining regions of northwestern India.<sup id="cite_ref-hbk_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbk-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> They are generally resident within their range for the whole year. In his 1950 work, <i>Grønlands Fugle</i> [<i>Birds of Greenland</i>], noted ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Finn_Salomonsen" title="Finn Salomonsen">Finn Salomonsen</a> indicated that common ravens did not overwinter in the Arctic.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> However, in Arctic Canada and Alaska, they are found year-round.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Young birds may disperse locally.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodwin._p139_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin._p139-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the common raven's range has been increasing, though it favours mountainous or coastal terrain, but can also be found in parks with tall trees suitable for use as habitation. Its population is at its most dense in the north and west of the country, though the species is expanding its population southwards.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most common ravens prefer wooded areas with large expanses of open land nearby, or coastal regions for their nesting sites and feeding grounds. In some areas of dense human population, such as <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> in the United States, they take advantage of a plentiful food supply and have seen a surge in their numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-mojave_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mojave-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> On coasts, individuals of this species are often evenly distributed and prefer to build their nest sites along sea cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-P._J._Ewins_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P._J._Ewins-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Common ravens are often located in coastal regions because these areas provide easy access to water and a variety of food sources.<sup id="cite_ref-P._J._Ewins_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P._J._Ewins-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Also, coastal regions have stable weather patterns without extreme cold or hot temperatures.
</p><p>In general, common ravens live in a wide array of environments but prefer heavily contoured landscapes. When the environment changes in vast degrees, these birds will respond with a stress response. The hormone known as <a href="/wiki/Corticosterone" title="Corticosterone">corticosterone</a> is activated by the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-John_F._Cockrem_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_F._Cockrem-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Corticosterone is activated when the bird is exposed to stress, such as migrating great distances.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_corax_2405_(8964295344).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Corvus_corax_2405_%288964295344%29.jpg/220px-Corvus_corax_2405_%288964295344%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Corvus_corax_2405_%288964295344%29.jpg/330px-Corvus_corax_2405_%288964295344%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Corvus_corax_2405_%288964295344%29.jpg/440px-Corvus_corax_2405_%288964295344%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_corax_2405_(8964295344).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group of ravens gathered around dead member</div></div></div>
<p>Common ravens usually travel in mated pairs, although young birds may form <a href="/wiki/Flock_(birds)" title="Flock (birds)">flocks</a>. Relationships between common ravens are often quarrelsome, yet they demonstrate considerable devotion to their families.<sup id="cite_ref-Oregon_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oregon-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predation">Predation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Predation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Owing to its size, gregariousness and its defensive abilities, the common raven has few natural predators. Predators of its eggs include <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a>, and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>. Ravens are quite vigorous at defending their young and are usually successful at driving off perceived threats. They attack potential predators by flying at them and lunging with their large bills. Humans are occasionally attacked if they get close to a raven nest, though serious injuries are unlikely. There are a few records of predation by large birds of prey. Their attackers in America have reportedly included <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawks</a>. It is possible that the two hawk species only attack young ravens; in one instance a <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> swooped at a newly fledged raven but was chased off by the parent ravens.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, their reported predators include, in addition to golden eagles, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea-eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Steller&#39;s sea-eagle">Steller's sea-eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Because they are potentially hazardous prey for raptorial birds, raptors must usually take them by surprise and most attacks are on fledgling ravens. More rarely still, large mammalian predators such as <a href="/wiki/Lynx" title="Lynx">lynxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">coyotes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cougar" title="Cougar">cougars</a> have also attacked ravens. This principally occurs at a nest site and when other prey for the carnivores are scarce. Ravens are highly wary around novel carrion sites and, in North America, have been recorded waiting for the presence of <a href="/wiki/American_crow" title="American crow">American crows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blue_jay" title="Blue jay">blue jays</a> before approaching to eat.<sup id="cite_ref-adw_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adw-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg/220px-Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg/330px-Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg/440px-Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ravens_nest_Lastef.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young on a nest – Hvítserkur, Iceland</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg/220px-Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg/330px-Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg/440px-Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4904" data-file-height="3369" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_corax_tingitanus_MHNT_232_HdB_Djebel_Messaad_Algerie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eggs of <i>Corvus corax</i></div></div></div>
<p>Juveniles begin to court at a very early age, but may not bond for another two or three years. Aerial acrobatics, demonstrations of intelligence, and ability to provide food are key behaviours of courting. Once paired, they tend to nest together for life, usually in the same location.<sup id="cite_ref-Oregon_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oregon-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Instances of non-monogamy have been observed in common ravens, by males visiting a female's nest when her mate is away.<sup id="cite_ref-Heinrich,_B._1999_pp&#160;119–120_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heinrich,_B._1999_pp_119–120-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Breeding pairs must have a territory of their own before they begin nest-building and reproduction, and thus aggressively defend a territory and its food resources. Nesting territories vary in size according to the density of food resources in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-Boarman_Heinrich_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boarman_Heinrich-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The nest is a deep bowl made of large sticks and twigs, bound with an inner layer of roots, mud, and bark and lined with a softer material, such as <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> fur. The nest is usually placed in a large tree or on a cliff ledge, or less frequently in old buildings or utility poles.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Females lay between three and seven pale bluish-green, brown-blotched eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-observerbook_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-observerbook-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Incubation is about 18 to 21 days, by the female only. The male may stand or crouch over the young, sheltering but not actually <a href="/wiki/Brooding" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooding">brooding</a> them.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Young <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> at 35 to 42 days, and are fed by both parents. They stay with their parents for another six months after fledging.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In most of their range, egg-laying begins in late February. In colder climates, it is later, e.g. April in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>. In Pakistan, egg-laying takes place in December.<sup id="cite_ref-Helm_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helm-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Eggs and hatchlings are preyed on, rarely, by large <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, large <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marten" title="Marten">martens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canid" class="mw-redirect" title="Canid">canids</a>. The adults, which are very rarely preyed upon, are often successful in defending their young from these predators, due to their numbers, large size and cunning.<sup id="cite_ref-adw_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adw-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> They have been observed dropping stones on potential predators that venture close to their nests.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common ravens can be very long-lived, especially in captive or protected conditions; <a href="/wiki/Ravens_of_the_Tower_of_London" title="Ravens of the Tower of London">individuals at the Tower of London</a> have lived for more than 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Boarman_Heinrich_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boarman_Heinrich-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Lifespans in the wild are considerably shorter at typically 10 to 15 years. The longest known lifespan of a <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">banded</a> wild common raven was 23 years, 3 months.<sup id="cite_ref-euroring_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euroring-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg/220px-Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg/330px-Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg/440px-Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2145" data-file-height="1430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_Raven_Nationalpark_Bayerischer_Wald_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Feeding</div></div></div>
<p>Common ravens are <a href="/wiki/Omnivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Omnivorous">omnivorous</a> and highly <a href="/wiki/Opportunism#Biological_opportunism" title="Opportunism">opportunistic</a>: their diet may vary widely with location, season and <a href="/wiki/Serendipity" title="Serendipity">serendipity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nogales_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nogales-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> For example, those foraging on <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_North_Slope" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic North Slope">Arctic North Slope</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> obtained about half their energy needs from <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>, mainly of <a href="/wiki/Microtus" title="Microtus">microtine rodents</a>, and half by scavenging, mainly of <a href="/wiki/Caribou" class="mw-redirect" title="Caribou">caribou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_ptarmigan" title="Rock ptarmigan">ptarmigan</a> carcasses.<sup id="cite_ref-Temple_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Temple-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some places they are mainly <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavengers</a>, feeding on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> as well as the associated <a href="/wiki/Maggot" title="Maggot">maggots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carrion_beetle" class="mw-redirect" title="Carrion beetle">carrion beetles</a>. With large-bodied carrion, which they are not equipped to tear through as well as birds such as hook-billed <a href="/wiki/Vulture" title="Vulture">vultures</a>, they must wait for the prey to be torn open by another predator or flayed by other means.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Plant food includes cereal grains, berries and fruit. They prey on small <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, amphibians, reptiles, small mammals and birds.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Ravens may also consume the undigested portions of animal feces, and human food waste. They store surplus food items, especially those containing fat, and will learn to hide such food out of the sight of other common ravens.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodwin._p139_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin._p139-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Ravens also raid the food caches of other species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> They sometimes associate with another canine, the <a href="/wiki/Grey_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey wolf">grey wolf</a>, as a <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kleptoparasite">kleptoparasite</a>, following to scavenge wolf-kills in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Ravens are regular predators at bird nests, brazenly picking off eggs, nestlings and sometimes adult birds when they spot an opportunity. They are considered perhaps the primary natural threat to the nesting success of the critically endangered <a href="/wiki/California_condor" title="California condor">California condor</a>, since they readily take condor eggs and are very common in the areas where the species is being re-introduced. On the other hand, when they defend their own adjacent nests, they may incidentally benefit condors since they chase <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> out of the area that may otherwise prey upon larger nestling and fledging condors. Condors, despite their large size, do not seem to have well developed nest defenses.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_raves_landfill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Common_raves_landfill.jpg/220px-Common_raves_landfill.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Common_raves_landfill.jpg/330px-Common_raves_landfill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Common_raves_landfill.jpg/440px-Common_raves_landfill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1264" data-file-height="840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Common_raves_landfill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flock feeding at a garbage dump</div></div></div>
<p>Common ravens nesting near sources of human <a href="/wiki/Food_waste" title="Food waste">garbage</a> included a higher percentage of food waste in their diet, birds nesting near roads consumed more <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">road-killed</a> <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, and those nesting far from these sources of food ate more <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> and plant material. <a href="/wiki/Fledging" class="mw-redirect" title="Fledging">Fledging</a> success was higher for those using human garbage as a food source.<sup id="cite_ref-Kristan_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kristan-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, a 1984–1986 study of common raven diet in an agricultural region of south-western <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> found that cereal grains were the principal constituent of <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">pellets</a>, though small mammals, grasshoppers, cattle carrion and birds were also eaten.<sup id="cite_ref-Engel_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engel-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One behaviour is recruitment, where juvenile ravens call other ravens to a food bonanza, usually a carcass, with a series of loud yells. In <i><a href="/wiki/Ravens_in_Winter" title="Ravens in Winter">Ravens in Winter</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Heinrich" title="Bernd Heinrich">Bernd Heinrich</a> posited that this behaviour evolved to allow the juveniles to outnumber the resident adults, thus allowing them to feed on the carcass without being chased away.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernd89_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernd89-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> A more mundane explanation is that individuals co-operate in sharing information about carcasses of large mammals because they are too big for just a few birds to exploit.<sup id="cite_ref-Heinrich,_B._1999_pp&#160;119–120_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heinrich,_B._1999_pp_119–120-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Experiments with baits however show that such recruitment behaviour is independent of the size of the bait.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, there has been research suggesting that the common raven is involved in seed dispersal. In the wild, the common raven chooses the best habitat and disperses seeds in locations best suited for its survival.<sup id="cite_ref-P._J._Ewins_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P._J._Ewins-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence">Intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bird_intelligence" title="Bird intelligence">Bird intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corvidae#Intelligence" title="Corvidae">Corvidae §&#160;Intelligence</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r960796168">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Crows, ravens, magpies, and jays are not just feathered machines, rigidly programmed by their genetics. Instead, they are beings that, within the constraints of their molecular inheritance, make complex decisions and show every sign of enjoying a rich awareness.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Candace_Savage" title="Candace Savage">Candace Savage</a><sup id="cite_ref-birdinblack_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdinblack-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The brain of the common raven is among the largest of any bird species. Specifically, their <a href="/wiki/Avian_pallium" title="Avian pallium">hyperpallium</a> is large for a bird. They display ability in problem-solving, as well as other <a href="/wiki/Cognitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> processes such as <a href="/wiki/Imitation" title="Imitation">imitation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insight_phenomenology" title="Insight phenomenology">insight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-birdinblack_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdinblack-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG/220px-Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG/330px-Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG/440px-Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_NIKE_Missile_radar_dome_with_ravens.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dilapidated <a href="/wiki/Project_Nike" title="Project Nike">NIKE Missile</a> radar dome in Alaska with an evening roost</div></div></div>
<p>Linguist <a href="/wiki/Derek_Bickerton" title="Derek Bickerton">Derek Bickerton</a>, building on the work of biologist <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Heinrich" title="Bernd Heinrich">Bernd Heinrich</a>, has argued that ravens are one of only four known animals (the others being <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, and humans) who have demonstrated <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(linguistics)" title="Displacement (linguistics)">displacement</a>, the capacity to communicate about objects or events that are distant in space or time from the communication. Subadult ravens roost together at night, but usually forage alone during the day. However, when one discovers a large carcass guarded by a pair of adult ravens, the unmated raven will return to the roost and communicate the find. The following day, a flock of unmated ravens will fly to the carcass and chase off the adults. Bickerton argues that the advent of linguistic displacement was perhaps the most important event in the evolution of human language, and that ravens are the only other <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> to share this with humans.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One experiment designed to evaluate insight and <a href="/wiki/Problem-solving" class="mw-redirect" title="Problem-solving">problem-solving</a> ability involved a piece of meat attached to a string hanging from a perch. To reach the food, the bird needed to stand on the perch, pull the string up a little at a time, and step on the loops to gradually shorten the string. Four of five common ravens eventually succeeded, and "the transition from no success (ignoring the food or merely yanking at the string) to constant reliable access (pulling up the meat) occurred with no demonstrable <a href="/wiki/Trial-and-error" class="mw-redirect" title="Trial-and-error">trial-and-error</a> learning." This supports the hypothesis that common ravens are 'inventors', implying that they can solve problems. Many of the feats of common ravens were formerly argued to be stereotyped innate behaviour, but it now has been established that their aptitudes for solving problems individually and learning from each other reflect a flexible capacity for intelligent insight unusual among non-human animals.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Another experiment showed that some common ravens could intentionally deceive their conspecifics.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common ravens have been observed calling <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolves</a> to the site of dead animals. The <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolves</a> open the carcass, leaving the scraps more accessible to the birds.<sup id="cite_ref-birdinblack_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birdinblack-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> They watch where other common ravens bury their food and remember the locations of each other's food caches, so they can steal from them. This type of theft occurs so regularly that common ravens will fly extra distances from a food source to find better hiding places for food.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> They have also been observed pretending to make a cache without actually depositing the food, presumably to confuse onlookers.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Common ravens are known to steal and cache shiny objects such as pebbles, pieces of metal, and golf balls. One theory is that they hoard shiny objects to impress other ravens.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Other research indicates that juveniles are deeply curious about all new things, and that common ravens retain an attraction to bright, round objects based on their similarity to bird eggs. Mature birds lose their intense interest in the unusual, and become highly <a href="/wiki/Neophobia" title="Neophobia">neophobic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Play">Play</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Play">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There has been increasing recognition of the extent to which birds engage in <a href="/wiki/Play_(activity)" title="Play (activity)">play</a>. Juvenile common ravens are among the most playful of bird species. They have been observed to slide down snowbanks, apparently purely for fun. They even engage in games with other species, such as playing catch-me-if-you-can with wolves, otters and dogs.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Common ravens are known for spectacular aerobatic displays, such as flying in loops or interlocking talons with each other in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>They are also one of only a few wild animals who make their own toys. They have been observed breaking off twigs to play with socially.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_and_management">Conservation and management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Conservation and management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Compared to many smaller <i><a href="/wiki/Corvus" title="Corvus">Corvus</a></i> species (such as <a href="/wiki/American_crow" title="American crow">American crow</a>), ravens prefer undisturbed mountain or forest habitat or rural areas over urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In other areas, their numbers have increased dramatically and they have become agricultural <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a>. Common ravens can cause damage to crops, such as nuts and grain, or can harm livestock, particularly by killing young goat kids, lambs and calves.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Ravens generally attack the faces of young livestock, but the more common raven behaviour of scavenging may be misidentified as predation by ranchers.<sup id="cite_ref-nass_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nass-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the western <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>, human settlement and land development have led to an estimated 16-fold increase in the common raven population over 25 years. Towns, landfills, sewage treatment plants and artificial ponds create sources of food and water for scavenging birds. Ravens also find nesting sites in utility poles and ornamental trees, and are attracted to roadkill on highways. The explosion in the common raven population in the Mojave has raised concerns for the <a href="/wiki/Desert_tortoise" title="Desert tortoise">desert tortoise</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">threatened species</a>. Common ravens prey upon juvenile tortoises, which have soft shells and move slowly.<sup id="cite_ref-mojave_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mojave-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Plans to control the population have included shooting and trapping birds, as well as contacting landfill operators to ask that they reduce the amount of exposed garbage.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> A hunting bounty as a method of control was historically used in Finland from the mid-18th century until 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Culling has taken place to a limited extent in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, where the population increase in common ravens is threatening the vulnerable <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_eider" title="Steller&#39;s eider">Steller's eider</a> (<i>Polysticta stelleri</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-MMS2007_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMS2007-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions">Cultural depictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cultural depictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bill_Reid_raven.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bill_Reid_raven.jpg/220px-Bill_Reid_raven.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bill_Reid_raven.jpg/330px-Bill_Reid_raven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bill_Reid_raven.jpg/440px-Bill_Reid_raven.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bill_Reid_raven.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bill_Reid" title="Bill Reid">Bill Reid</a>'s sculpture <i>The Raven and The First Men</i>, showing part of a <a href="/wiki/Haida_mythology" title="Haida mythology">Haida</a> creation myth. Museum of Anthropology, <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">University of British Columbia</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_ravens" title="Cultural depictions of ravens">Cultural depictions of ravens</a></div>
<p>Across its range in the Northern Hemisphere, and throughout human history, the common raven has been a powerful symbol and a popular subject of mythology and folklore.
</p><p>In some <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western traditions</a>, ravens have long been considered to be birds of ill omen, death and evil in general, in part because of the negative symbolism of their all-<a href="/wiki/Black#Beliefs,_religions_and_superstitions" title="Black">black</a> plumage and the eating of <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, ravens are known as the ghosts of murdered people, and in Germany as the souls of the damned. In <a href="/wiki/Danish_folklore" title="Danish folklore">Danish folklore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valravn" title="Valravn">valravne</a> that ate a king's heart gained human knowledge, could perform great malicious acts, could lead people astray, had superhuman powers, and were "terrible animals".<sup id="cite_ref-KRISTENSEN132_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KRISTENSEN132-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As in traditional mythology and folklore, the common raven features frequently in more modern writings such as the works of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, and, perhaps most famously, in the poem "<a href="/wiki/The_Raven" title="The Raven">The Raven</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>. Ravens have appeared in the works of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_King" title="Stephen King">Stephen King</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_R._R._Martin" title="George R. R. Martin">George R. R. Martin</a><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Joan_Aiken" title="Joan Aiken">Joan Aiken</a><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> among others.
</p><p>It continues to be used as a symbol in areas where it once had mythological status: as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_birds" title="List of national birds">national bird</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a><sup id="cite_ref-ravenbhutan_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ravenbhutan-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Druk_Gyalpo" title="Druk Gyalpo">Kings of Bhutan</a> wear the <a href="/wiki/Raven_Crown" title="Raven Crown">Raven Crown</a>), official bird of the <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a> territory,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> and on the coat of arms of the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> (once a Viking colony).<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Persia and Arabia the raven was held as a bird of bad omen but a 14th-century Arabic work reports use of the raven in falconry.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern <a href="/wiki/Unisex_given_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Unisex given name">unisex given name</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Raven_(given_name)" title="Raven (given name)">Raven</a></i> is derived from the English word "raven". As a masculine name, <i>Raven</i> parallels the Old Norse <i><a href="/wiki/Hrafn" title="Hrafn">Hrafn</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Hanks-226_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hanks-226-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> and Old English <i>*Hræfn</i>, which were both <a href="/wiki/Byname" class="mw-redirect" title="Byname">bynames</a> and <a href="/wiki/Personal_name" title="Personal name">personal names</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Reaney_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reaney-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mythology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Raven_in_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Raven in mythology">Raven in mythology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Valkyrie_and_Raven_(NGV).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Valkyrie_and_Raven_%28NGV%29.png/170px-Valkyrie_and_Raven_%28NGV%29.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Valkyrie_and_Raven_%28NGV%29.png/255px-Valkyrie_and_Raven_%28NGV%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Valkyrie_and_Raven_%28NGV%29.png/340px-Valkyrie_and_Raven_%28NGV%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="2009" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Valkyrie_and_Raven_(NGV).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Valkyrie" title="Valkyrie">valkyrie</a> speaks with a raven in a 19th-century illustration of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> poem <i><a href="/wiki/Hrafnsm%C3%A1l" title="Hrafnsmál">Hrafnsmál</a></i> ("raven song") by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Sandys" title="Frederick Sandys">Frederick Sandys</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Tlingit#Creation_story_and_the_Raven_Cycle" title="History of the Tlingit">Tlingit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haida_mythology" title="Haida mythology">Haida cultures</a>, Raven was both a <a href="/wiki/Trickster" title="Trickster">trickster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Creator_god" class="mw-redirect" title="Creator god">creator god</a>. Related beliefs are widespread among the peoples of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and northeastern Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka_Peninsula" title="Kamchatka Peninsula">Kamchatka Peninsula</a>, for example, was supposed to have been created by the raven god <a href="/wiki/Kutkh" title="Kutkh">Kutkh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> There are several references to common ravens in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and it is an aspect of <a href="/wiki/Mahakala" title="Mahakala">Mahakala</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutanese</a> mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-ravenbhutan_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ravenbhutan-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huginn_and_Muninn" title="Huginn and Muninn">Huginn</a> (from the Old Norse for "thought") and <a href="/wiki/Huginn_and_Muninn" title="Huginn and Muninn">Muninn</a> (from the Old Norse for "memory" or "mind") are a pair of ravens that fly all over the world of humans, <a href="/wiki/Midgard" title="Midgard">Midgard</a>, and bring the god <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a> information. Additionally among the Norse, <a href="/wiki/Raven_banner" title="Raven banner">raven banner</a> standards were carried by such figures as the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Orkney" title="Earl of Orkney">Jarls of Orkney</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> King <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut the Great</a> of England, Norway and Denmark,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Harald_Hardrada" title="Harald Hardrada">Harald Hardrada</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In the British Isles, ravens also were symbolic to the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Irish_mythology" title="Irish mythology">Irish mythology</a>, the goddess <a href="/wiki/Morr%C3%ADgan" class="mw-redirect" title="Morrígan">Morrígan</a> alighted on the hero <a href="/wiki/C%C3%BA_Chulainn" title="Cú Chulainn">Cú Chulainn</a>'s shoulder in the form of a raven after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Welsh_mythology" title="Welsh mythology">Welsh mythology</a> they were associated with the Welsh god <a href="/wiki/Bran_the_Blessed" class="mw-redirect" title="Bran the Blessed">Bran the Blessed</a>, whose name translates to "raven." According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Mabinogion" title="Mabinogion">Mabinogion</a></i>, Bran's head was buried in the White Hill of London as a <a href="/wiki/Talisman" title="Talisman">talisman</a> against invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A legend developed that England would not fall to a foreign invader as long as there were <a href="/wiki/Ravens_of_the_Tower_of_London" title="Ravens of the Tower of London">ravens at the Tower of London</a>; although this is often thought to be an ancient belief, the official Tower of London historian, Geoff Parnell, believes that this is actually a romantic Victorian invention.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> traditions, the raven was the first animal to be released from <a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark" title="Noah&#39;s Ark">Noah's Ark</a>. "So it came to pass, at the end of forty days, that Noah opened the window of the ark which he had made. Then he sent out a raven, which kept going to and fro until the waters had dried up from the earth. He also sent out from himself a dove, to see if the waters had receded from the face of the ground."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The raven is mentioned 12 times in the Bible. In the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus</a> tells a parable using the raven to show how people should rely on God for their needs and not riches (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/Luke#12:24" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/Luke">Luke 12:24</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The raven is also mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> at the story of <a href="/wiki/Cain_and_Abel" title="Cain and Abel">Cain and Abel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a>'s firstborn son Cain kills his brother Abel, but he does not know what to do with the corpse: "Then Allah sent a raven scratching up the ground, to show him how to hide his brother's naked corpse. He said: Woe unto me! Am I not able to be as this raven and so hide my brother's naked corpse? And he became repentant."<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Common_raven&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
